Fighting Invalidates Prayer If Excessive Action
Commentary on ruling that fighting invalidates prayer with distinction between actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fighting during prayer and distinguish between excessive (amal kathir) and minimal (amal qalil) actions that invalidate or preserve the prayer.

Core Idea
Only excessive action invalidates prayer; a single throw does not.

This lesson examines a key ruling on actions during prayer: fighting. The text clarifies that not all fighting nullifies the prayer—only excessive bodily movement does. A single throw (ramyah) is considered minimal and does not invalidate. A hadith from the Battle of the Trench supports this, and the principle extends to analogous cases like a swimmer unable to control his limbs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is fighting during prayer. The ruling: if the fighting involves excessive action (amal kathir), the prayer is invalid (batil). If the action is minimal (amal qalil), like a single throw, the prayer remains valid.
The key is distinguishing the quantity and nature of movement.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith of al-Mughira: during the Battle of the Trench, the Prophet ﷺ was occupied with fighting and delayed four prayers until after nightfall. If fighting were permissible during prayer, he would not have delayed them. This indicates that active combat nullifies prayer.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), but its implication (dalala) is considered strong enough for a ruling.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The proof lies in the Prophet's action (delaying prayers). By postponing the prayers, he demonstrated that fighting and prayer cannot coexist. This is an implicit proof (dalala) that fighting invalidates the prayer—otherwise he would have prayed on time while fighting.
The dalala is zanni; it does not explicitly state invalidation but implies it.
4
Apply to the swimmer case
The author extends the principle: a swimmer who cannot control his limbs due to the current should not pray, and if he does, his prayer is invalid. If he can manage slight movement, he prays with gestures. This analogy shows that the criterion is the inability to avoid excessive movement.
This is a qiyas on the same illa: excessive involuntary action disrupting the prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ومن قاتل بطلت صلاتة ; لأنه عمل كثير مفسد للصلاة وهو مراده بالمقاتلة وإلا فلو قاتل بعمل قليل كالرمية لا تفسد كما علم في مفسدات الصلاة. واستدل في المجتبى بحديث المغيرة أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم شغل عن أربع صلوات يوم الخندق فصلاهن من بعد ما مضى من الليل ولو جاز مع القتال لما أخرهن عن وقتهن ا.هـ. وأشار المصنف إلى أن السابح في البحر إذا لم يمكنه أن يرسل أعضاءه ساعة فإنه لا يصلي فإن صلى لا تصح، وإن أمكنه ذلك فإنه يصلي بالإيماء كذا في المجتبى
He said: (And whoever fights, his prayer becomes invalid) because it is excessive action that nullifies prayer, and that is what is meant by fighting; otherwise, if one fights with a small action like a single throw, it does not invalidate, as is known from the nullifiers of prayer. And he adduced evidence in al-Mujtaba from the hadith of al-Mughira that the Prophet ﷺ was occupied from four prayers on the day of al-Khandaq and prayed them after the night had passed; if fighting had been permissible, he would not have delayed them beyond their times. And the author indicated that the swimmer in the sea if he cannot move his limbs for a moment, then he should not pray, and if he prays it is not valid; but if he can, then he prays with gestures, as in al-Mujtaba.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement 'Whoever fights, his prayer becomes invalid.' The author explains that 'fighting' here means excessive bodily movement that nullifies prayer. If the action is small, like a single throw (رمية), it does not invalidate. This is established in the nullifiers of prayer. The author then cites a hadith from al-Mughira: during the Battle of the Trench, the Prophet ﷺ delayed four prayers until after nightfall; if fighting had been permissible during prayer, he would not have delayed them. This proves that active combat invalidates prayer. The commentator also draws an analogy with a swimmer in the sea: if he cannot control his limbs (due to the current), he should not pray, and his prayer would be invalid; but if he can manage slight movement, he prays with gestures (imā').

Key Terms
عمل كثير (amal kathir): Excessive bodily movement that disrupts the form of prayer, such as multiple steps or vigorous actions.
رمية (ramyah): A single throw or shot; a minimal action that does not invalidate prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following actions during prayer would invalidate it according to this passage?
A) Throwing a single spear at the enemy
B) Engaging in hand-to-hand combat with multiple strikes
C) Moving one step forward
D) Absorbing a hit without moving

Key Takeaway
Excessive bodily movement (amal kathir) during prayer invalidates it, while minimal actions (amal qalil) like a single throw do not.

Extreme Fear Prayer: Conditions for Praying on Horseback with Gestures
Commentary on extreme fear prayer: conditions, proof, and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which praying on horseback with gestures is valid and identify scenarios where the dispensation does not apply.

Core Idea
Praying on horseback with gestures is valid only when extreme fear prevents dismounting — the concession is for necessity, not convenience.

In extreme fear, such as battle or flight, the Shari‘ah grants concessions for prayer. This lesson analyzes the Hanafi ruling: an individual may pray on horseback, making gestures for bowing and prostration, facing any direction. But this dispensation has strict conditions — it is not a blanket permission. We examine the proof, the limits, and the exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the rating
The act is the obligatory prayer performed on horseback using gestures (imā’) when fear is severe. The ruling is that it is valid (sahih) under the stated conditions.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The author cites Quran 2:239: {فإن خفتم فرجالاً أو ركباناً} (If you fear, then pray on foot or riding). This verse is the basis for the dispensation.
The verse is qat‘ī al-thubūt (certain transmission) but its dalāla (indication) is zannī as it requires interpretation.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalālah
The verse permits prayer 'on foot or riding', which implies that full standing, bowing to the ground, and facing the qibla are not required when fear prevents them. Gesturing (īmā’) substitutes for rukū‘ and sujūd, and qibla direction is waived.
4
List the conditions for the dispensation
The dispensation requires: (1) extreme fear such that dismounting is impossible; (2) the prayer must be performed individually (unless sharing the same mount with the imam); (3) it must be outside a populated city; (4) the person must be pursued (a pursuer does not have the excuse). Walking prayer is invalid because walking is many actions.
The text also clarifies that the movement of the animal is not attributed to the rider; only the rider's intention and gestures matter.
5
Identify the ikhtilāf and limitations
The prayer cannot be performed in congregation unless all are on the same mount. In a city, prayer on a mount is invalid even in fear. A pursuer may not pray on horseback because he is not in fear.
These limitations ensure the dispensation is not abused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإذا اشتد الخوف صلوا ركباناً فرادى بالإيماء إلى أي جهة قدروا لقوله تعالى {فإن خفتم فرجالاً أو ركباناً} [البقرة: 239] والتوجه إلى القبلة للضرورة أراد بالاشتداد أن لا يتهيأ لهم النزول عن الدابة كما في غاية البيان. قيد بقوله «فرادى» لأنه لا يجوز بجماعة لعدم الاتحاد في المكان إلا إذا كان راكباً مع الإمام على دابة واحدة فإنه يجوز اقتداء المتأخر منهما بالمتقدم اتفاقاً. ويرد على المصنف ما إذا صلى راكباً في المصر فإنه لا يجوز إلا أن يقال : إنه معلوم مما قدمه من أن التطوع لا يجوز في المصر راكباً فكذا الفرض للضرورة. وقيد بالركوب لأنه لا يجوز ماشياً في غير المصر لأن المشي عمل كثير مفسد للصلاة كالغريق السابح كما قدمناه. وفي المحيط والراكب إن كان طالباً لا يجوز صلاته على الدابة لعدم ضرورة الخوف في حقه وإن كان مطلوباً فلا بأس أن يصلي وهو سائر لأن السير فعل الدابة حقيقة وإنما أضيفت إليه معنى بتسييره فإذا جاء العذر انقطعت الإضافة إليه بخلاف ما لو صلى وهو يمشي حيث لا يجوز لأن المشي فعله حقيقة وهو مناف للصلاة اهـ
He said: (And when fear intensifies, they pray as riders individually, making gestures towards whatever direction they can) due to the saying of Allah تعالى: {If you fear, then [pray] on foot or riding} [al-Baqarah 2:239]. And facing the qibla is waived due to necessity. By 'intensification' he means they are unable to dismount from the animal, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. He restricted by saying 'individually' because congregational prayer is not allowed due to lack of unity in place, except if they are riding with the imam on a single animal, then it is permissible for the one behind to follow the one in front, by agreement. And an objection is raised against the author concerning one who prays riding in a city, which is not permissible; but it can be said that it is known from what he previously stated that voluntary prayer is not allowed riding in a city, so the same applies to obligatory prayer out of necessity. And he restricted to riding because walking is not allowed outside the city, since walking is excessive action that nullifies prayer, like the drowning swimmer as we mentioned. And in al-Muhit: the rider, if he is a pursuer, his prayer on the animal is not allowed because there is no fear necessity in his case; but if he is pursued, then there is no harm in him praying while moving, because the movement is actually that of the animal, and is only attributed to him metaphorically due to his driving it; when there is an excuse, the attribution ceases. Unlike if he prays while walking, which is not allowed, because walking is actually his action and is incompatible with prayer.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage comments on the ruling for extreme fear: individuals pray on horseback, alone, making gestures (imā') for bowing and prostration, and they may face any direction. The proof is Quran 2:239. The condition for this dispensation is that dismounting is impossible. 'Individually' (فرادى) means they cannot pray in congregation unless they share the same mount with the imam. The commentator addresses an apparent contradiction: praying on a mount in the city is generally not allowed, but the author's principle is that voluntary prayer on a mount in the city is invalid, so obligatory prayer out of necessity is similarly invalid there; the exception is only for actual fear outside the city. Walking prayer is invalid because walking is a lot of action. Furthermore, a pursuer (one chasing the enemy) does not have the excuse of fear, so he cannot pray on the animal; but one being pursued may pray while moving, as the movement is attributed to the animal, not to him.

Key Terms
إيماء (imā’): Gesturing with the head or body for bowing and prostration instead of full movements, allowed in extreme fear or illness.
فرادى (furādā): Individually, i.e., each person prays alone, not in congregation.
طالب (tālib): Pursuer; one chasing the enemy. In this context, a pursuer does not have the excuse of extreme fear.
مطلوب (matlūb): Pursued; one fleeing from the enemy. Their prayer on mount is valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what condition makes a pursuer's prayer on horseback invalid?
A) He is moving too fast
B) He does not have the excuse of extreme fear
C) He is not facing qibla
D) He is riding a horse instead of a camel

Key Takeaway
Prayer on horseback with gestures is valid only for the pursued under extreme fear where dismounting is impossible; a pursuer or one in a city must pray normally.

Fear Prayer Invalid Without Enemy Presence
Commentary on fear prayer condition and mistake case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for valid fear prayer in the Hanafi school and apply the exception of istihsan when mistaken identity is discovered early.

Core Idea
The fear prayer requires actual enemy presence; subjective fear is insufficient.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on ṣalāt al-khawf. The school insists that the concession is tied to the actual presence of the enemy, not the worshipper's perception of danger. We will explore the reasoning behind this, including an important case where a mistaken identification leads to a repetition of the prayer, and the role of istiḥsān in preserving the validity of the prayer when the error is caught early.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Identify the act under discussion: ṣalāt al-khawf (fear prayer). The ruling from the matn: it is not valid without actual enemy presence. The core idea: the cause of the concession is the cause (sabab) of fear, not fear itself.
The text explicitly states 'ولم تجز بلا حضور عدو'.
2
Locate the dalil
The reasoning is based on a principle established in travel concessions: the shortening of prayer is attached to the journey itself, not the hardship experienced. Similarly, fear prayer is attached to the presence of the enemy, not the subjective feeling of fear. This is a qiyas (analogy) to the travel ruling.
The author writes: 'حضرة العدو أقيمت مقام الخوف على ما عرف في أصلنا في تعليق الرخصة بنفس السفر لا حقيقة المشقة'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
The legal link: just as a journey is the cause for the concession of shortening, the enemy's presence is the cause for the concession of fear prayer. Therefore, without the cause, the concession does not apply.
The presence of the enemy is the sabab; the actual fear is the hikmah (wisdom). The ruling is tied to the sabab.
4
Note the ikhtilāf
The Hanafi position is that fear prayer is only valid with actual enemy presence. Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) allow fear prayer based on genuine fear from any source, such as flood or fire. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
The cross_madhhab_status in the hanafi_position_box is 'mukhtalaf_fih'.
5
Apply the nuance
If people mistakenly think a black mass is the enemy and pray the fear prayer, they must repeat unless they realize before crossing the rows (i.e., while still in formation), in which case they may continue by istihsan. Also, the imam's prayer is always valid because no invalidating act occurs on his part.
The exception of istihsan is a key nuance that relaxes the strict ruling when the mistake is realized early.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حضرة العدو أقيمت مقام الخوف على ما عرف في أصلنا في تعليق الرخصة بنفس السفر لا حقيقة المشقة، لأن السفر سبب المشقة فأقيم مقامها فكذا حضرة العدو هنا سبب الخوف فأقيم مقامه حقيقة الخوف ا هـ. وفي فتح القدير: واعلم أن صلاة الخوف على الصفة المذكورة إنما تلزم إذا تنازع القوم في الصلاة، أما إذا لم يتنازعوا فالأفضل أن يصلي بإحدى الطائفتين تمام الصلاة ويصلي بالطائفة الأخرى إمام آخر تمامها ا هـ. وذكر الأسبيجابي أن انصرف منهم إلى وجه العدو راكباً فإنه لا يجوز، سواء كان انصرافه من القبلة إلى العدو وعكسه
قوله : (ولم تجز بلا حضور عدو لعدم الضرورة حتى لو أراد سواداً فظنوا أنه عدو فصلوا صلاة الخوف ثم بان أنه ليس بعد وأعادوها لما قلنا إلا إذا بان لهم قبل أن يتجاوزوا الصفوف فإن لهم أن يبنوا استحساناً، وهذا كله في حق القوم، وأما الإمام فصلاته جائزة بكل حال لعدم المفسد في حقه والله أعلم.
The presence of the enemy is treated as fear, as is known in our principle regarding attaching the concession to the journey itself, not the actual hardship, because journey is the cause of hardship, so it is treated as such; similarly here, the presence of the enemy is the cause of fear, so it is treated as the reality of fear. And in Fath al-Qadir: Know that the fear prayer in the described form is only necessary when the people dispute about the prayer; otherwise, it is better that the imam prays the complete prayer with one group and another imam leads the other group completely. And al-Asbījābī mentioned that if one of them turns towards the enemy while riding, it is not allowed, whether he turns from the qibla to the enemy or vice versa. He said: (And it is not valid without the presence of the enemy) because of lack of necessity, such that if they see a black mass and think it is the enemy, and they perform the fear prayer, then it becomes clear that it is not [the enemy], they repeat it, for what we said; except if it becomes clear to them before they cross the rows, then they may build upon it by istihsan. And all this is regarding the people; as for the imam, his prayer is valid in any case because there is no invalidating factor on his part. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling that the fear prayer is only valid when the enemy is actually present, because the concession is based on the cause of fear (the enemy's presence), not on the subjective feeling of fear. The author cites Fath al-Qadir: the fear prayer in the described form (with two groups) is only necessary if the people are disputing about the prayer (i.e., they cannot agree on a single method); otherwise, it is better to pray fully with each group separately. Al-Asbījābī adds that turning towards the enemy while riding during prayer invalidates it. Then the author returns to the matn: the fear prayer is not valid without an actual enemy. If people mistake a black mass for the enemy and pray in fear, then realize it was not the enemy, they must repeat. However, if they realize before crossing the rows (i.e., while still in the formation), they may continue on the fear prayer method by istihsan. The imam's prayer is always valid because no invalidating action occurs on his part.

Key Terms
حضرة العدو (hadrat al-‘adū): The actual presence of the enemy, which is the cause for the concession of fear prayer.
استحسان (istihsān): Juristic preference in Hanafi fiqh used to relax a strict ruling; here allowing them to continue the fear prayer when the mistake is realized early.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is the fear prayer not valid?
A) When the enemy is not actually present
B) When there is a flood
C) When the imam is leading
D) When they pray as riders

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school restricts ṣalāt al-khawf to situations where the enemy is physically present; subjective fear alone does not justify the concession.

Prayer on a Mount: The Pursuer vs. The Pursued
Explaining the ruling on prayer while riding for pursuer and pursued

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the rulings for a pursuer (talib) and a pursued (matlub) when praying on a mount, and explain the legal attribution of the mount's movement.

Core Idea
The pursued may pray while riding; the pursuer may not—necessity determines attribution of action.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq clarifies a key distinction in the prayer of flight. The Hanafi school differentiates between the pursuer and the pursued based on necessity. The movement of the mount is legally attributed to the rider only when there is a valid excuse, such as being chased. This lesson unpacks that attribution and its implications for validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage divides riders into two types: talib (pursuer) and matlub (pursued). Recognize the Arabic terms.
Talib is actively chasing; matlub is fleeing.
2
State the ruling for each
The talib's prayer on a mount is not permissible due to lack of necessity. The matlub's prayer is permissible because fear provides necessity.
3
Analyze the attribution of movement
The movement is actually the mount's action, not the rider's. It is attributed to the rider only in the sense of direction. With an excuse (fear), the attribution ceases.
This is a key Hanafi principle: actions of the mount are not considered the rider's actions for the purpose of invalidating prayer.
4
Contrast with walking
If the rider prays while walking, the prayer is invalid because walking is his own direct action, which is excessive movement.
Walking is classified as 'amal kathir (excessive action) that invalidates prayer.
5
Apply the principle
Necessity (udhr) is the condition that allows the mount's movement to be overlooked. Without necessity, the rukhsa is not granted.
The ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) allowing the prayer despite movement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عمل كثير مفسد للصلاة كالغريق السابح كما قدمناه. وفي المحيط والراكب إن كان طالباً لا يجوز صلاته على الدابة لعدم ضرورة الخوف في حقه وإن كان مطلوباً فلا بأس أن يصلي وهو سائر لأن السير فعل الدابة حقيقة وإنما أضيفت إليه معنى بتسييره فإذا جاء العذر انقطعت الإضافة إليه بخلاف ما لو صلى وهو يمشي حيث لا يجوز لأن المشي فعله حقيقة وهو مناف للصلاة اهـ . قوله : (ولم تجز بلا حضور عدو لعدم الضرورة حتى لو أراد سواداً فظنوا أنه عدو فصلوا صلاة الخوف ثم بان أنه ليس بعد وأعادوها لما قلنا إلا إذا بان لهم قبل أن يتجاوزوا الصفوف فإن لهم أن يبنوا استحساناً، وهذا كله في حق القوم، وأما الإمام فصلاته جائزة بكل حال لعدم المفسد في حقه والله أعلم.
Excessive action invalidates prayer, like a drowning swimmer as we have stated. And in al-Muhit: the rider, if he is a pursuer, his prayer on the mount is not permissible due to the absence of necessity of fear in his case; but if he is being pursued, there is no harm in him praying while moving, because movement is actually the action of the mount, and it is attributed to him only in the sense of its being directed by him. When there is an excuse, the attribution to him ceases, contrary to if he prays while walking, which is not permissible because walking is his own action and it contradicts the prayer. End quote. His statement: (And it is not permissible without the presence of an enemy due to lack of necessity — even if they saw a black mass and thought it was an enemy and prayed the prayer of fear, then it became clear it was not an enemy, they would repeat it for what we said, unless it became clear to them before they passed beyond the rows, for then they may build upon it by istihsan. All this is concerning the group. As for the imam, his prayer is valid in any case because there is no invalidating factor in his case. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of prayer during flight (salat al-firar). The author distinguishes between the pursuer (talib) and the pursued (matlub). For the pursuer, prayer on a mount is not valid because there is no necessity — he is voluntarily chasing the enemy. For the pursued, it is permissible because the movement is attributed to the mount, not to him directly; the motion is excused due to necessity. Walking during prayer is not allowed because walking is directly his action and is excessive movement. The passage then addresses a scenario where people mistake a dark mass for an enemy and perform salat al-khawf. If they later discover it was not an enemy, they must repeat the prayer (according to the principle that the condition of danger was absent). However, if they realize the mistake before leaving the rows (i.e., while still in the prayer formation), they may continue on the basis of istihsan (juristic preference) because the fear was genuine at the start. The imam's prayer is always valid because he is not required to perform specific actions like the followers.

Key Terms
مطلوب (matlūb): One who is being pursued by an enemy
طالب (ṭālib): One who is pursuing an enemy
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that allows building on a prayer that began with a valid excuse even if the excuse disappears mid-prayer

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the prayer of a pursued rider valid while that of a pursuer is not?
A) The pursued rider is in a state of fear which overrides all invalidating actions.
B) The movement is attributed to the mount, and necessity allows the prayer to be valid.
C) The pursued rider is allowed to do any action during prayer due to necessity.
D) The pursuer's prayer is invalid only if he walks.

Key Takeaway
Necessity allows the movement of the mount to be legally separated from the rider's prayer, but only for the pursued, not the pursuer.

Mistaken Fear and the Ruling of Istihsan
Explaining the ruling when fear is mistakenly perceived

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of istihsan when a mistaken fear is discovered during prayer, and distinguish the imam's special status.

Core Idea
If the enemy turns out to be imaginary, the prayer must be repeated—unless discovered while still in formation, then istihsan allows building.

This passage also addresses a scenario where people see a dark mass and think it is an enemy, performing salat al-khawf. Later they discover it was not an enemy. The Hanafi ruling distinguishes between discovering before leaving the rows (permits building by istihsan) and after (requires repetition). The imam's prayer is always valid because he has no invalidating action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage considers a group that mistakes a dark mass (sawad) for an enemy and performs the prayer of fear (salat al-khawf).
The dark mass could be a tree, animal, etc.
2
State the default ruling
If they later discover it was not an enemy, they must repeat the prayer because the essential condition (presence of enemy) was absent.
The prayer was invalid from the start because the fear was based on a mistaken perception.
3
Identify the exception via istihsan
If they realize the mistake before they pass beyond the rows (i.e., before breaking the prayer formation), they may continue their prayer and complete it by istihsan.
Istihsan considers the initial valid excuse as continuing even after the excuse disappears, as long as they haven't left the prayer configuration.
4
Explain the imam's exemption
The imam's prayer is valid in any case because there is no invalidating factor (mafsid) in his actions—he does not perform the specific movements of the followers that might be excessive.
The imam remains stationary; the followers move around him in the fear prayer formation.
5
Contrast with the group
The group's prayer depends on the validity of the excuse; the imam's does not. This shows a hierarchy of permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عمل كثير مفسد للصلاة كالغريق السابح كما قدمناه. وفي المحيط والراكب إن كان طالباً لا يجوز صلاته على الدابة لعدم ضرورة الخوف في حقه وإن كان مطلوباً فلا بأس أن يصلي وهو سائر لأن السير فعل الدابة حقيقة وإنما أضيفت إليه معنى بتسييره فإذا جاء العذر انقطعت الإضافة إليه بخلاف ما لو صلى وهو يمشي حيث لا يجوز لأن المشي فعله حقيقة وهو مناف للصلاة اهـ . قوله : (ولم تجز بلا حضور عدو لعدم الضرورة حتى لو أراد سواداً فظنوا أنه عدو فصلوا صلاة الخوف ثم بان أنه ليس بعد وأعادوها لما قلنا إلا إذا بان لهم قبل أن يتجاوزوا الصفوف فإن لهم أن يبنوا استحساناً، وهذا كله في حق القوم، وأما الإمام فصلاته جائزة بكل حال لعدم المفسد في حقه والله أعلم.

Responsibility for the Shroud of a Deceased with No Wealth
Intra-Hanafi dispute on husband's obligation for wife's kafan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for who must provide the shroud when the deceased has no wealth, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the husband's obligation.

Core Idea
The shroud duty follows the maintenance duty: whoever was obliged in life is obliged after death.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on Kanz al-Daqaiq, establishing a key principle: the obligation of shrouding a deceased who has no wealth falls upon the persons who were responsible for their maintenance (nafaqah) during life. It then delves into an important disagreement within the Hanafi school: Is the husband compelled to shroud his wife? Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani differ, and the fatwa follows Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
The shroud obligation mirrors the maintenance obligation: whoever was obligated to provide nafaqah and clothing during life is responsible for the kafan after death.
This is based on qiyas: the deceased's need for covering continues.
2
Note the Specific Applications
Examples include: slave's shroud on master, mortgaged slave's shroud on mortgagor, sold goods in seller's possession on seller. If no such person, Bayt al-Mal; if none, Muslim community; if unable, solicit donations.
The rule is applied consistently across different relationships.
3
Recognize the Distinction from the Living
A living person without a garment for prayer is not entitled to solicited donations because he can ask for himself, whereas the deceased cannot.
This highlights the difference in capability (qudra).
4
Isolate the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that the husband is compelled to shroud his wife by analogy with maintenance; Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, arguing maintenance ends at death.
Both are within the Hanafi school; Abu Hanifa's view is not explicitly recorded.
5
Trace the Tarjih
Al-Walwalji and the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq prefer Abu Yusuf's view, making it the mufta bihi. The reasoning includes the analogy with the master-slave relationship.
Later fatwa works rely on Abu Yusuf's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجنائز
الخانية لأنه ككسوتها والكسوة واجبة عليه غنية كانت أو فقيرة غنياً كان أو فقيراً. وصححه الولوالجي في فتاواه من النفقات فإن لم يكن للميت مال فكفنه على من تجب عليه نفقته وكسوته في حياته. وكفن العبد على سيده والمرهون على الراهن والمبيع في يد البائع عليه، فإن لم يكن له من تجب النفقة عليه فكفنه في بيت المال، فإن لم يكن فعلى المسلمين تكفينه، فإن لم يقدر واسألوا الناس ليكفنوه بخلاف الحي إذا لم يجد ثوباً يصلي فيه ليس على الناس أن يسألوا له ثوباً، والفرق أن الحي يقدر على السؤال بنفسه والميت عاجز، فإن سألوا له وفضل من الكفن شيء يرد إلى المتصدق، وإن لم يعلم يتصدق به على الفقراء اعتباراً بكسوته . كذا في المجتبى. وفي التجنيس والواقعات : إذا لم يعلم المتصدق يكفن به مثله من أهل الحاجة وإن لم يتيسر يصرف إلى الفقراء. وفيهما : لو كفن ميتاً ، ماله من ثم وجد الكفن فله أن يأخذه وهو أحق به لأن الميت لم يملكه. وفيهما: حي عريان وميت ومعهما ثوب واحد فإن كان للحي فله لبسه ولا يكفن به الميت لأنه محتاج إليه وإن كان ملك الميت والحي وارثه يكفن به الميت ولا يلبسه لأن الكفن مقدم على الميراث، وإذا تعدد من وجبت النفقة عليه على ما يعرف في النفقات فالكفن عليهم على قدر ميراثهم كما كانت النفقة واجبة عليهم، ولو مات معتق شخص ولم يترك شيئاً وله خالة موسرة يؤمر معتقه بتكفينه . وقال محمد على خالته وفي الخانية من لا يجبر على النفقة في حياته كأولاد الأعمام والعمات والأخوال والخالات لا يجبر على الكفن . زاد في الظهيرية : وإن كان وارثاً. وفي البدائع : ولا يجب على المرأة كفن زوجها بالإجماع كما لا يجب عليها كسوته في الحياة. وفي القنية : ولو مات ولا شيء له وجب كفنه على ورثته فكفنه الحاضر من مال نفسه ليرجع على الغائب منهم بحصتهم ليس له الرجوع إذا أنفق عليه بغير إذن القاضي قال محمد رحمه الله : كالعبد أو الزرع أو النخل بين شريكين أنفق أحدهما عليه ليرجع على الغائب لا يرجع إذا فعله بغير إذن القاضي ا هـ .

أمير حاج في شرح المنيه حيث قال : ينبغي أن يكون محل الخلاف ما إذا لم يقم بها مانع يمنع الوجوب عليه حالة الموت من نشوز أو صغر مع كبره ونحو ذلك .ا.هـ. قوله : (وصححه الولوالجي في فتاواه النفقات أقول : الذي رأيته في نفقات الولوالجية هكذا إذا ماتت المرأة ولا مال لها قال أبو يوسف : يجبر الزوج على كفنها. والأصل فيه أن من يجبر على نفقته في حال حياته يجبر على نفقته بعد موته كذوي الأرحام والعبد مع المولى والزوجة مع الزوج. وقال محمد: لا يجبر الزوج على كفنها. والصحيح قول أبي يوسف لأن المولى إنما يجبر على تكفين العبد لأن كان أولى به في حالة حياته فيكون أولى بإيجاب الكفن عليه من بين سائر الناس وهذا المعنى موجود هنا ا هـ .

Book of Funerals. Al-Khaniyya: because it is like her clothing, and clothing is obligatory upon him whether she is rich or poor, whether he is rich or poor. And Al-Walwalji authenticated it in his Fatawa from the Nafaqat. If the deceased has no wealth, then his shroud is upon the one who was obligated to provide maintenance and clothing for him during his lifetime. The shroud of a slave is upon his master; the shroud of a mortgaged [slave] is upon the mortgagor; the shroud of a sold item in the hand of the seller is upon the seller. If he has no one upon whom maintenance was obligatory, then his shroud is from the Bayt al-Mal. If there is none, then it is upon the Muslims to shroud him. If they cannot, let them ask the people to shroud him. Unlike a living person if he cannot find a garment to pray in, it is not upon the people to ask for a garment for him. The difference is that the living person is able to ask for himself, while the deceased is incapable. If they ask for him and something remains from the shroud, it is returned to the donor; if it is not known, it is given in charity to the poor, considering it as his clothing. Thus in Al-Mujtaba. In Al-Tajnis and Al-Waqi'at: if the donor is not known, he is shrouded with its like from among those in need, and if that is not possible, it is given to the poor. In both: if someone shrouded a deceased and then his property is found and the shroud is found, he may take it and he has more right to it because the deceased did not own it. And in both: a living person naked and a deceased with one garment; if the garment belongs to the living, he may wear it and the deceased is not shrouded with it because he needs it; if it is owned by the deceased and the living is his heir, the deceased is shrouded with it and he does not wear it because the shroud takes precedence over inheritance. And if there are multiple persons upon whom maintenance was obligatory, as known in Nafaqat, then the shroud is upon them according to the shares of inheritance, just as maintenance was obligatory upon them. If a person's freed slave died and left nothing, and he has a maternal aunt who is affluent, the one who freed him is ordered to shroud him. Muhammad said: it is upon his maternal aunt. In Al-Khaniyya: those who are not compelled to provide maintenance during life, like paternal uncles' children, paternal aunts, maternal uncles, and maternal aunts, are not compelled to provide the shroud. In Al-Zahiriyya, it added: even if they are heirs. In Al-Bada'i': it is not obligatory upon a woman to shroud her husband by consensus, just as it is not obligatory upon her to provide his clothing during life. In Al-Qunyah: if he died with nothing, his shroud is obligatory upon his heirs; so the present heir shrouds him from his own wealth to claim back from the absent heir their share; he does not have the right to claim if he spent without the judge's permission. Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: like a slave, or a crop, or a palm tree shared between two partners; if one of them spends on it to claim back from the absent, he does not have the right to claim if he did so without the judge's permission. End. From Amir Hajj in Sharh al-Munyah, who said: the place of disagreement should be when no preventing factor prevents the obligation at the time of death, such as nushuz or being a minor with old age, etc. End. His statement: (And Al-Walwalji authenticated it in his Fatawa from the Nafaqat). I say: what I have seen in the Nafaqat of Al-Walwalji is as follows: If a woman dies and has no wealth, Abu Yusuf said: the husband is compelled to shroud her. The basis for this is that whoever is compelled to provide maintenance during life is compelled to provide after his death, like relatives, a slave with his master, and a wife with her husband. Muhammad said: the husband is not compelled to shroud her. The correct position is the statement of Abu Yusuf, because the master is compelled to shroud his slave because he was most entitled to him during life, so he is more entitled to imposing the shroud upon him than other people, and this meaning is present here. End. And since the husband is compelled to provide maintenance for his wife during her life even if he is poor, he is also compelled to shroud her. His statement: (His shroud is obligatory, etc.) which is in Al-Qunyah, with the word "wajaba" with two waws, the first being for conjunction.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses the obligation of providing the kafan (shroud) for a deceased person who has no wealth. It is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq. The passage first establishes a general principle: the duty to shroud the deceased falls upon the same person who was obligated to provide for their maintenance (nafaqah) and clothing during life. This is based on qiyas: just as maintenance is obligatory in life, so is the shroud after death. The text gives specific examples: a slave's shroud is on his master; a mortgaged slave's shroud on the mortgagor; and goods sold but still in the seller's possession, the shroud is on the seller. If no such person exists, the state treasury (Bayt al-Mal) bears the cost; if none, the Muslim community; if they cannot afford it, they may solicit donations. A distinction is made: a living person in need of a garment for prayer is not entitled to such solicitation because he can ask for himself, whereas the deceased cannot. Any leftover from donated cloth is returned to the donor if known, otherwise given in charity to the poor. The text then presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute: whether the husband is compelled to shroud his wife who dies without wealth. Abu Yusuf holds that he is, by analogy with his obligation of maintenance during life. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that he is not, because the husband's obligation of maintenance ends with death. The text sides with Abu Yusuf, citing Al-Walwalji's fatwa and the reasoning that just as a master must shroud his slave because he is most entitled to him in life, so the husband is most entitled to his wife. The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is that the husband is obliged to provide the kafan even if he is poor. The passage also clarifies that a wife is not obliged to shroud her husband by consensus. Finally, it addresses cases of multiple heirs or absent heirs, noting that the heir who shrouds from his own pocket can only claim reimbursement from co-heirs with the judge's permission.

Key Terms
كفن (kafan): The shroud or funeral cloth used to wrap the deceased body.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance, including food, clothing, and shelter, which is obligatory for certain relatives and spouses.
إجبار (ijbar): Compulsion by the judge to fulfill a financial obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school, who is obligated to provide the kafan for a deceased wife who has no wealth?
A) The wife's father
B) The husband
C) The wife's children
D) The Bayt al-Mal

Key Takeaway
The obligation to shroud a deceased without wealth falls on the same persons who were responsible for their maintenance; the husband is compelled to shroud his wife even if poor.

The Sultan's Right to Lead the Funeral Prayer
Establishing who has the greatest right to lead the funeral prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the order of precedence for leading the janazah prayer as per the relied-upon Hanafi position and identify the conditions for its validity.

Core Idea
The sultan's right to lead the janazah prayer is absolute; his deputies follow in hierarchical order.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the matn 'al-sultan ahaqq bi salatihi'. The commentator explains that 'sultan' includes all rulers, not just the caliph, and provides a detailed order of precedence that is the mufta bihi in the school. You will learn the chain of authority from the caliph down to the deputies and the conditions for leading the prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Extract the basic principle: The ruler (sultan) has the greatest right to lead the janazah prayer when present.
2
Define 'Sultan'
Understand that in Hanafi fiqh, 'sultan' includes any ruler with general authority (caliph, governor, etc.), not just the highest leader.
3
List the Detailed Order
Based on Abu Ja'far's transmission, the order is: caliph → deputy of the city → judge → police chief → their deputies.
This is the mukhtar (preferred) order.
4
Note the Conditions
The janazah prayer is fard kifayah; its conditions include Islam of the deceased and purification. The order of precedence is a matter of right (ahaqqiyya), not obligation.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The detailed order is the relied-upon position according to Fath al-Qadir and al-Khulasa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل السلطان أحق بصلاته وهي فرض كفاية وشرطها إسلام الميت وطهارته ثم إمام الحي ثم فصل السلطان أحق بصلاته يعني إذا حضر لأن في التقدم عليه استخفافاً به ولما مات الحسن قدم الحسين سعيد بن العاص وقال : لولا السنة ما قدمتك أطلق في السلطان وأراد به من له سلطنه أي حكم وولاية على العامة، سواء كان الخليفة أو غيره، فيقدم الخليفة إن حضر ثم نائب المصر ثم القاضي ثم صاحب الشرط ثم خليفته ثم خليفة القاضي وهذا ما نقله الفقيه أبو جعفر، والإمام الفضلي إنما نقل تقديم السلطان وهو الخليفة فقط وأما من عداه فليس له التقدم على الأولياء إلا برضاهم. قال في الظهيرية والخانية : إنه قياس قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف وزفر فعلى هذا فالمراد من السلطان في المختصر هو الوالي الذي لا والي فوقه لكن المذكور في المحيط والبدائع والتبيين والمجمع وشرحه التفصيل المتقدم عن أبي جعفر واقتصر عليه في فتح القدير وصرح في الخلاصة بأنه المختار فكان هو المذهب. وقدم أبو يوسف الولي . رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة وما في الأصل من أن إمام الحي أولى بها فمحمول على ما إذا لم يحضر السلطان ولا من يقوم مقامه توفيقاً بينهما لأن السلطان قل ما يحضر الجنائز . كذا فصل السلطان أحق بصلاته ا هـ.
ذلك مطلقاً وهو قوله : (سعيد بن العاص لأنه كان والياً على المدينة كما في الفتح قوله : (فعلى هذا فالمراد من السلطان الخ) حاصله أن كلام المصنف يحتمل إجراؤه على كل من القولين، ورده في النهر بأنه غير لقوله بعد «ثم القاضي وعطف الخاص على العام شرطه الواو ا.هـ. وحاصله أنه على كلامه صحيح لا يحتمل أن يكون على القول الثاني لأنه ذكر القاضي بعده ولا على الأول لعطفه إياه بـ«ثم» ولا يكون في عطف الخاص على العام ثم قال : والتحقيق أن المراد به إمام المصر ومنه يعلم تقديم الإمام الأعظم بالأولى ا هـ. وفي تخصيصه عطف الخاص على العام بالواو نظر فإنه يكون بـ«حتى» نحو مات الناس حتى الأنبياء نص عليه في مغني الليبب، بل قد جوزه بعض المحققين بـ«ثم» أيضاً واستدل له بحديث إن الله كتب الإحسان على كل شيء فإذا قتلتم فأحسنوا القتلة وإذا ذبحتم فأحسنوا الذبحة ثم ليرح ذبيحته وليحد أحدكم شفرته ) (١) وقد وقع بـ «أو» أيضاً كما في الحديث ومن كانت هجرته إلى دنيا يصيبها أو امرأة يتزوجها
Section: The Sultan is most entitled to his (the deceased's) funeral prayer. It is a communal obligation, and its conditions are the Islam of the deceased and his purification. Then the imam of the locality, then... Section: The Sultan is most entitled to his funeral prayer, meaning when he is present, because preceding him (in leading) is a slight to him. When al-Hasan died, al-Husayn gave precedence to Sa'id ibn al-'As and said: 'Were it not for the sunna, I would not have given you precedence.' He used the term 'sultan' generally, meaning one who has authority and governance over the public, whether the caliph or others. So the caliph if present takes precedence, then the deputy of the city, then the judge, then the chief of police, then his deputy, then the deputy of the judge. This is what the jurist Abu Ja'far transmitted. Al-Imam al-Fadli only transmitted giving precedence to the sultan, i.e., the caliph alone, and as for others, they do not have precedence over the guardians (awliya') except with their consent. It is said in al-Zahiriyya and al-Khaniyya: it is the qiyas (analogical deduction) of the statements of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar. Based on this, the meaning of 'sultan' in al-Mukhtasar is the governor who has no governor above him. However, what is mentioned in al-Muhit, al-Bada'i', al-Tabyin, al-Majma' and its commentary is the detailed order from Abu Ja'far, and Fath al-Qadir confined himself to it, and al-Khulasa explicitly stated that it is the chosen view, so it is the madhhab. Abu Yusuf gave precedence to the wali (guardian). In the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa, and what is in al-Asl that the imam of the locality is more entitled to it (the funeral prayer) is interpreted as when the sultan and his deputies are not present, as a reconciliation between them, because the sultan rarely attends funerals. Thus ends the section: the Sultan is most entitled to his funeral prayer. That is absolute, and his statement: (Sa'id ibn al-'As because he was the governor of Medina, as in al-Fath). His statement: (Based on this, the meaning of sultan, etc.) Its summary is that the author's speech can be applied to both views. Al-Nahr refuted it because it is not [applicable] due to his saying later 'then the judge', and the conjunction of the specific with the general requires 'waw'. Its summary is that his speech is correct and cannot be applied to the second view because he mentioned the judge after it, nor to the first view because he conjoined it with 'thumma', and conjunction of the specific with the general does not occur with 'thumma'. Then he said: The verification is that what is meant is the imam of the city, and from this it is known that the greater imam is given precedence with more reason. In his specification of the conjunction of the specific with the general by 'waw' there is consideration, for it occurs with 'hatta' as in 'people died, even the prophets', as stated in Mughni al-Labib. Rather, some investigators allowed it with 'thumma' as well, and they adduced as evidence the hadith: 'Verily Allah prescribed ihsan (excellence) in everything; so when you kill, do well in the killing; when you slaughter, do well in the slaughter; then let each of you sharpen his blade and let him comfort his animal.' And it has occurred with 'aw' as in the hadith: 'Whoever emigrates for a worldly gain or a woman he marries...'
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq deals with the question of who has the priority to lead the funeral prayer (salat al-janazah). The matn states that the Sultan (ruler) is most entitled, then the imam of the locality, etc. The commentator clarifies that 'sultan' means anyone with authority over the public, including the caliph, governors, etc. There is a dispute within the Hanafi school about the exact order. According to the transmission of Abu Ja'far, the order is: the caliph (if present), then the deputy of the city, then the judge, then the chief of police, then his deputy, then the deputy of the judge. This detailed order is preferred by many Hanafi authorities including the authors of al-Muhit, al-Bada'i', al-Tabyin, al-Majma', and Fath al-Qadir. Another view, attributed to al-Imam al-Fadli, restricts 'sultan' to the caliph only, and all others are on par with the ordinary guardians (awliya') and can only lead with their consent. Abu Yusuf is reported to have given precedence to the wali (the guardian, e.g., the closest relative) over the sultan's deputies. The text reconciles the various narrations by interpreting a statement from al-Asl that 'the imam of the locality is more entitled' as referring to situations where the sultan or his representatives are absent. The relied-upon (madhhab) position is the detailed order of Abu Ja'far. The passage also includes a discussion about the grammatical conjunction used in the matn (thumma, waw, etc.) and its implications for the order of precedence.

Key Terms
سلطان (sultan): The ruler or person with supreme authority (caliph or governor) who has the right to lead the funeral prayer when present.
ولي (wali): The guardian of the deceased, typically the closest male relative (e.g., son, father) who has the right to lead the funeral prayer by default.
إمام الحي (imam al-hayy): The imam of the locality or neighborhood, who leads the five daily prayers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the detailed order preferred in the Hanafi school, who leads the funeral prayer after the caliph?
A) The judge (qadi)
B) The chief of police (sahib al-shurta)
C) The deputy of the city (na'ib al-misr)
D) The guardian (wali) of the deceased

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position for precedence in janazah prayer is the detailed hierarchical order transmitted by Abu Ja'far: caliph, then deputy, then judge, then police chief, then their deputies, with the wali after.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Deputies vs. Guardian in Janazah Leadership
Establishing who has the greatest right to lead the funeral prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and al-Imam al-Fadli regarding precedence in janazah prayer and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
The sultan's deputies do not automatically outrank the deceased's guardian in all Hanafi views; the mukhtar position gives deputies precedence.

While the matn states 'al-sultan ahaqq', the scope of 'sultan' and the rank of deputies relative to the wali is disputed within the Hanafi school. This lesson explores the differing positions and their practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Two Main Views
Summarize: (1) Abu Ja'far's detailed order gives precedence to deputies; (2) al-Imam al-Fadli restricts 'sultan' to the caliph, so deputies need wali's consent.
2
Understand the Basis of Each View
View 1 relies on qiyas to avoid disrespect to authority. View 2 relies on common linguistic usage and protecting the wali's right.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's Position
Abu Yusuf gave precedence to the wali over deputies, suggesting a middle ground.
4
Identify the Tarjih Mechanism
Later Hanafi authorities (Fath al-Qadir, al-Khulasa) preferred Abu Ja'far's order as the mukhtar.
5
Apply to a Practical Scenario
When a judge and police chief are present at a funeral, according to the mufta bihi, the judge leads before the police chief, and deputies lead before the wali.

فصل السلطان أحق بصلاته وهي فرض كفاية وشرطها إسلام الميت وطهارته ثم إمام الحي ثم فصل السلطان أحق بصلاته يعني إذا حضر لأن في التقدم عليه استخفافاً به ولما مات الحسن قدم الحسين سعيد بن العاص وقال : لولا السنة ما قدمتك أطلق في السلطان وأراد به من له سلطنه أي حكم وولاية على العامة، سواء كان الخليفة أو غيره، فيقدم الخليفة إن حضر ثم نائب المصر ثم القاضي ثم صاحب الشرط ثم خليفته ثم خليفة القاضي وهذا ما نقله الفقيه أبو جعفر، والإمام الفضلي إنما نقل تقديم السلطان وهو الخليفة فقط وأما من عداه فليس له التقدم على الأولياء إلا برضاهم. قال في الظهيرية والخانية : إنه قياس قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف وزفر فعلى هذا فالمراد من السلطان في المختصر هو الوالي الذي لا والي فوقه لكن المذكور في المحيط والبدائع والتبيين والمجمع وشرحه التفصيل المتقدم عن أبي جعفر واقتصر عليه في فتح القدير وصرح في الخلاصة بأنه المختار فكان هو المذهب. وقدم أبو يوسف الولي . رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة وما في الأصل من أن إمام الحي أولى بها فمحمول على ما إذا لم يحضر السلطان ولا من يقوم مقامه توفيقاً بينهما لأن السلطان قل ما يحضر الجنائز . كذا فصل السلطان أحق بصلاته ا هـ.
ذلك مطلقاً وهو قوله : (سعيد بن العاص لأنه كان والياً على المدينة كما في الفتح قوله : (فعلى هذا فالمراد من السلطان الخ) حاصله أن كلام المصنف يحتمل إجراؤه على كل من القولين، ورده في النهر بأنه غير لقوله بعد «ثم القاضي وعطف الخاص على العام شرطه الواو ا.هـ. وحاصله أنه على كلامه صحيح لا يحتمل أن يكون على القول الثاني لأنه ذكر القاضي بعده ولا على الأول لعطفه إياه بـ«ثم» ولا يكون في عطف الخاص على العام ثم قال : والتحقيق أن المراد به إمام المصر ومنه يعلم تقديم الإمام الأعظم بالأولى ا هـ. وفي تخصيصه عطف الخاص على العام بالواو نظر فإنه يكون بـ«حتى» نحو مات الناس حتى الأنبياء نص عليه في مغني الليبب، بل قد جوزه بعض المحققين بـ«ثم» أيضاً واستدل له بحديث إن الله كتب الإحسان على كل شيء فإذا قتلتم فأحسنوا القتلة وإذا ذبحتم فأحسنوا الذبحة ثم ليرح ذبيحته وليحد أحدكم شفرته ) (١) وقد وقع بـ «أو» أيضاً كما في الحديث ومن كانت هجرته إلى دنيا يصيبها أو امرأة يتزوجها
Section: The Sultan is most entitled to his (the deceased's) funeral prayer. It is a communal obligation, and its conditions are the Islam of the deceased and his purification. Then the imam of the locality, then... Section: The Sultan is most entitled to his funeral prayer, meaning when he is present, because preceding him (in leading) is a slight to him. When al-Hasan died, al-Husayn gave precedence to Sa'id ibn al-'As and said: 'Were it not for the sunna, I would not have given you precedence.' He used the term 'sultan' generally, meaning one who has authority and governance over the public, whether the caliph or others. So the caliph if present takes precedence, then the deputy of the city, then the judge, then the chief of police, then his deputy, then the deputy of the judge. This is what the jurist Abu Ja'far transmitted. Al-Imam al-Fadli only transmitted giving precedence to the sultan, i.e., the caliph alone, and as for others, they do not have precedence over the guardians (awliya') except with their consent. It is said in al-Zahiriyya and al-Khaniyya: it is the qiyas (analogical deduction) of the statements of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar. Based on this, the meaning of 'sultan' in al-Mukhtasar is the governor who has no governor above him. However, what is mentioned in al-Muhit, al-Bada'i', al-Tabyin, al-Majma' and its commentary is the detailed order from Abu Ja'far, and Fath al-Qadir confined himself to it, and al-Khulasa explicitly stated that it is the chosen view, so it is the madhhab. Abu Yusuf gave precedence to the wali (guardian). In the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa, and what is in al-Asl that the imam of the locality is more entitled to it (the funeral prayer) is interpreted as when the sultan and his deputies are not present, as a reconciliation between them, because the sultan rarely attends funerals. Thus ends the section: the Sultan is most entitled to his funeral prayer. That is absolute, and his statement: (Sa'id ibn al-'As because he was the governor of Medina, as in al-Fath). His statement: (Based on this, the meaning of sultan, etc.) Its summary is that the author's speech can be applied to both views. Al-Nahr refuted it because it is not [applicable] due to his saying later 'then the judge', and the conjunction of the specific with the general requires 'waw'. Its summary is that his speech is correct and cannot be applied to the second view because he mentioned the judge after it, nor to the first view because he conjoined it with 'thumma', and conjunction of the specific with the general does not occur with 'thumma'. Then he said: The verification is that what is meant is the imam of the city, and from this it is known that the greater imam is given precedence with more reason. In his specification of the conjunction of the specific with the general by 'waw' there is consideration, for it occurs with 'hatta' as in 'people died, even the prophets', as stated in Mughni al-Labib. Rather, some investigators allowed it with 'thumma' as well, and they adduced as evidence the hadith: 'Verily Allah prescribed ihsan (excellence) in everything; so when you kill, do well in the killing; when you slaughter, do well in the slaughter; then let each of you sharpen his blade and let him comfort his animal.' And it has occurred with 'aw' as in the hadith: 'Whoever emigrates for a worldly gain or a woman he marries...'
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq deals with the question of who has the priority to lead the funeral prayer (salat al-janazah). The matn states that the Sultan (ruler) is most entitled, then the imam of the locality, etc. The commentator clarifies that 'sultan' means anyone with authority over the public, including the caliph, governors, etc. There is a dispute within the Hanafi school about the exact order. According to the transmission of Abu Ja'far, the order is: the caliph (if present), then the deputy of the city, then the judge, then the chief of police, then his deputy, then the deputy of the judge. This detailed order is preferred by many Hanafi authorities including the authors of al-Muhit, al-Bada'i', al-Tabyin, al-Majma', and Fath al-Qadir. Another view, attributed to al-Imam al-Fadli, restricts 'sultan' to the caliph only, and all others are on par with the ordinary guardians (awliya') and can only lead with their consent. Abu Yusuf is reported to have given precedence to the wali (the guardian, e.g., the closest relative) over the sultan's deputies. The text reconciles the various narrations by interpreting a statement from al-Asl that 'the imam of the locality is more entitled' as referring to situations where the sultan or his representatives are absent. The relied-upon (madhhab) position is the detailed order of Abu Ja'far. The passage also includes a discussion about the grammatical conjunction used in the matn (thumma, waw, etc.) and its implications for the order of precedence.

Key Terms
سلطان (sultan): The ruler or person with supreme authority (caliph or governor) who has the right to lead the funeral prayer when present.
ولي (wali): The guardian of the deceased, typically the closest male relative (e.g., son, father) who has the right to lead the funeral prayer by default.
إمام الحي (imam al-hayy): The imam of the locality or neighborhood, who leads the five daily prayers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which view did Abu Yusuf hold regarding the precedence of the wali?
A) The wali outranks all deputies
B) The wali is equal to the caliph
C) The wali comes after all deputies
D) The wali leads only with the caliph's permission

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on janazah precedence is resolved by the mufta bihi: the detailed order of Abu Ja'far gives deputies precedence over the wali, while Abu Yusuf and al-Imam al-Fadli hold differing views.
